**Food.** Underprioritized but not disordered. Breakfast is often skipped or replaced by a banana, a protein bar, or whatever leftover starch is available. Lunch is takeout — bodega sandwich, halal cart, the slice place near the office — eaten at the desk, on the move, or standing. Dinner is the meal he treats with respect: when he eats alone he cooks (eggs, rice and beans, a competent chicken thigh, sometimes a callback to a dish his mother made that he will not name to others), and when he eats with others he is attentive, generous, the first to offer a refill. Surveillance would note that *he eats slowly when alone and quickly when with people who are eating slowly* — an attunement habit, lining his pace up with the room.

He drinks water through the day from a refillable bottle on the desk; the bottle is washed weekly, which puts him in a particular subset of New York men.

**Alcohol.** Cautious in Epoch A — the proximity to a recovering identity is felt — and increasingly normalized in Epoch B in social contexts, but rarely past three drinks and never to obvious impairment in public. He watches his own glass. He has the recovering person's quiet vigilance about other people's pours.

**Substances post-Kilgrave.** No opioid relapse, on record or by reasonable inference. No cocaine, no benzos, no recreational pills. He has, once or twice, accepted a joint passed in a private setting; he does not pursue cannabis. The deliberate negative space around all altered states is the loudest single fact about his ingestion file.

**Caffeine and nicotine.** No tobacco use. Vape-curious but not a user.

---

## 4. DRESS, GROOMING, AND THE BODY HE PRESENTS

**Wardrobe Epoch A.** Plain. Henleys, hoodies, jeans, a few button-downs for occasions, sneakers, a single decent jacket. The aesthetic reads as a graduate student who has not unpacked. Clean, inoffensive, functional, anonymous in the way an investigator benefits from being anonymous.

**Wardrobe Epoch B.** Deliberate upgrade. Better fabrics, a real coat, leather shoes maintained, a watch, dark slim trousers, a shirt that fits. He learns to dress for Hogarth's offices and once he learns he does not unlearn. The wardrobe in this period is *armor*; he is dressing toward the rooms he is being asked to enter, and the dressing-up tracks the moral compromise with eerie tightness. A surveillance analyst could plot his ethical drift against his dry-cleaning receipts.

**Grooming.** Hair kept short, edged regularly (every 10–14 days at the same shop once established; he is a loyal client and tips well). Beard line maintained. Nails clean and trimmed — this never lapses, in any epoch, which is consistent with a man who once felt his body controlled and now insists, in this small register, on owning it. He showers at night more often than morning, and the night shower is long: ten to fifteen minutes, often with the bathroom light dimmed or off, a habit that did not exist before Kilgrave and is therefore noted.

**Posture.** Open chest, easy shoulders by default; under stress, a closing-in — shoulders forward, one arm crossing the torso, the other hand at the mouth or jaw. He touches his own face often when thinking: chin, lower lip, the back of the neck. He blinks slowly when collecting himself.

**Gait.** Long stride, unhurried, deceptively quick because of the length. Hands often in jacket pockets, head up. He looks at people on the street — a behavior atypical for New York and entirely typical for him; it is residual helper-pattern, the trained eye of someone who once intended to read people for a living.

---

## 5. PLACE: THE APARTMENT, THE OFFICE, THE BLOCK

**Home (the Hell's Kitchen building, apartment opposite Jessica's).** A small one-bedroom or studio-plus, kept neat to the point of pattern. He makes the bed. He puts dishes in the sink rather than on surfaces. The coffee table has, at any given time: a laptop (closed when not in use), a notebook, a paperback, a coaster, a mug. Books visible on a low shelf: psychology paperbacks (a Yalom, a Herman *Trauma and Recovery* on the second shelf, undisturbed in placement which suggests it is *referenced* and *avoided* in roughly equal measure), a study Bible from his family, a few novels, a couple of Lonely Planet guides for trips he has not taken.

Walls largely bare; one or two framed pieces, a family photograph kept on a side surface rather than on display — visible to him from the couch, less visible to guests. A houseplant that he keeps alive, barely, by remembering it on Sundays.

The apartment door is locked, deadbolt and chain, every time, even for two-minute trips to the hallway. This is not premorbid behavior. This is post-Kilgrave behavior. Sound of the chain sliding home is one of the most consistent audio signatures in any surveillance file on him.

**The office (Alias Investigations, then Cheng's, then Hogarth's adjacent spaces).** At Alias, he occupies whatever surface Jessica is not currently using; he organizes the case files, makes coffee, keeps a notebook of his own. The office under his hand becomes noticeably tidier; under Jessica's, returns to entropy. The push-pull is daily and the dynamic is its own ritual.

At Cheng's: a desk, a phone, a chair. He uses them with restraint and discomfort. He never personalizes the desk.

At Hogarth's: a real workstation, well-appointed, used with growing fluency. He acquires office things — a good pen, a leather portfolio, a second monitor — that he would not have acquired in Epoch A and that he leaves behind, unreturned-to, by the end of S3.

**The block.** He knows it. The bodega owner knows him by face and order; the building super by name. He greets the unhoused man two doors down without performance and gives money irregularly but not for show. He notices new faces on the block within a beat or two — a hypervigilant reflex that he experiences as social warmth and that is also surveillance.

---

## 6. THE WORKING DAY

**Morning at the office.** Arrives 09:00–10:00 in Epoch A; 08:30 in Epoch B once professional employers structure his calendar. First action on arrival: coffee, then a five-to-ten-minute scan of any open cases — physical files in Epoch A, screens in Epoch B. He writes a short to-do list by hand in a small notebook each morning, even when there is also a digital task system; the handwritten list is talismanic.

**Field work.** Two registers. (a) The watcher: long stretches in cars, on benches, in coffee shops with sight lines, taking photographs. He is patient with this in a way few are — likely the gift of having been forced to do it for over a year and now doing it freely, which converts trauma into mastery. He drinks one coffee per setup and one bottle of water; he urinates *before* setup, never during; he keeps his phone face-down on the seat. (b) The talker: door-knocks, interviews, the persuasion of strangers. He is unusually effective at this. He opens by listening; he uses the person's name once, never twice; he reflects affect back accurately; he leaves the conversation slightly before the person wants him to. These are clinical-interview skills repurposed.

**Meetings with principals (Jessica / Cheng / Hogarth).** He arrives early. He stands when they enter. He takes the worse chair. He nods, he summarizes, he commits to deliverables and meets them. With Jessica he allows himself sarcasm and pushback; with Cheng he is correct; with Hogarth he is quietly attentive and increasingly silent across S3 — the silence of a man holding something in.

**Lunch.** Often skipped or eaten at the desk. If eaten out, alone, with his phone. He does not enjoy lunch.

**Afternoon.** Reporting, write-ups, follow-through. He is the rare investigator who actually writes the report on the day of the event. Documentation, for him, is partly professional and partly *exculpatory* — a record-keeping habit that hardens through S3 as the work becomes harder to defend.

**End of day.** Late, irregular. He often closes the office or is the last to leave. The walk home is performed deliberately as decompression: phone in pocket, no podcast for the first half, sometimes a single circle around the block before going upstairs to extend the transition.

---

## 7. EVENING AND THE PRIVATE HOUR

**Door, deadbolt, chain.** Drops keys in a particular dish. Removes shoes by the door. Removes jacket, hangs it.

**Decompression.** Showers (long, low light, as noted), changes into soft clothes. Eats, often alone, often standing at the counter at first and then sitting properly with a plate as a self-correction. Television is rarely on for its own sake; he uses it as a wash of sound while doing something else. If he watches something, it is documentaries, prestige drama, or basketball.

**Reading.** Reliable evening behavior. Twenty to forty minutes on the couch, paperback in hand. He reads slowly. He underlines occasionally with a pencil. He sometimes closes the book and sits with it on his chest for several minutes before resuming.

**Phone.** He calls his mother on a roughly weekly schedule, usually Sunday afternoon or evening; the call lasts 20–40 minutes; he is gentler and younger in this call than at any other moment in the surveillance week. He does not tell her what he does for a living in any meaningful detail. After the call he often stays still for a stretch.

He texts Jessica more than Jessica texts him. He composes long messages and sends short ones. The drafts folder of his messaging app, on a forensic capture, would be the document of record for the file.

**Sex and intimacy.** Episodic. He is capable of warmth and connection; he is also capable of using sex as task or as soothing. The Trish episode is paradigmatic — sex deployed instrumentally with cost he registers and dismisses. With partners more generally he is courteous, attentive, generous in bed, withdrawn after — leaves rather than stays, or stays and rises early. He has not, on canon, formed a sustained romantic partnership across the surveillance window.

**Late evening.** Increasingly, in Epoch B, work bleeds into late hours — laptop on the couch, second wind of coffee, case notes. The boundary between office and home erodes across the canonical period in lockstep with the moral erosion at work.

---

## 8. THE WEEK AS A SHAPE

A surveillance week, presented schematically:

- **Monday.** Heaviest work intake. Long day. Arrives early, leaves late, eats badly. Sleep onset deferred.
- **Tuesday.** Field-heavy. Mobile most of the day; sometimes the haircut/edge-up appointment if due. Evening often quiet.
- **Wednesday.** Office-heavy. Reporting, internal meetings, calls. Survivor-group convenings, in Epoch A, often fall mid-week — a regular evening obligation he protects against work intrusion.
- **Thursday.** Variable. Often the day a case breaks or a moral problem surfaces, which is a function of his calendar density rather than the universe's malice.
- **Friday.** Wind-down attempt that usually fails. Drinks with one or two people if invited, alone at home if not. Likeliest night to over-stay at the office.
- **Saturday.** The day he most resembles his pre-Kilgrave self. Sleeps later. Walks the neighborhood. Sometimes a long coffee at a particular spot with a book. Laundry. Groceries (actual cooking-grade groceries, not bodega supplements). The most observably *young* day of his week.
- **Sunday.** The family-call day. Also the day he reads more, organizes the apartment, considers — and usually does not attend — a church service. In Epoch A he attends occasionally; in Epoch B he does not, though the consideration persists. Sunday evenings carry a recognizable low affect — the Sunday scaries, but inflected with moral accounting rather than work dread.

---

## 9. RITUALS, MICRO-RITUALS, AND SUPERSTITIONS

- **The thirty-second pause** at the edge of the bed each morning before standing.
- **The two-minute brush.** Phone timer or visual check.
- **The bathroom prayer**, Epoch A; the mirror look that replaces it, Epoch B.
- **The handwritten to-do list**, every working morning, in the same small notebook, transferred to a new notebook when the previous fills (kept; not discarded).
- **The pre-setup urination and the no-mid-setup interruption** — a professional discipline that is also a refusal of bodily compulsion ever again.
- **The chain on the door.**
- **The shoes by the door.**
- **The Sunday call home.**
- **The single beer that is never two.**
- **The drafts folder** of unsent messages, particularly to Jessica.
- **The closing of the laptop screen** when he steps away from it, even in his own apartment with no one to see — a residue of having been watched and watching, and an insistence on a private interior.
- **The notebook that survives him from job to job.** Different employers, different desks; same notebook in the bag.
- **The houseplant on Sundays.** Watered as a marker of weeks elapsing.
- **The careful washing of the water bottle, weekly.**
- **The Yalom and the Herman** on the second shelf, unmoved, consulted.

---

## 10. WHAT SURVEILLANCE WOULD ULTIMATELY CONCLUDE

A week's footage on Malcolm Ducasse would mislead a casual analyst into reading a competent, well-organized, slightly lonely young professional with above-average self-care and below-average sleep. A careful analyst would see, beneath that, the silhouette of the captivity: the deadbolt, the closed laptop, the wardrobe-as-armor, the night shower in the dark, the prayer that thinned into a mirror, the drafts folder, the notebook that does not change. Nothing in his days is loud. Everything in his days is *organized against* a year that was loud, and against the lingering possibility — registered every time he locks the door — that it could be loud again.

He lives the way a person lives when they have decided, without announcing it to themselves, that the smallest unit of recovery is the well-kept hour. He keeps the hours. The hours, in turn, keep him.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

## Preface to the Section

The family record on Malcolm Ducasse is, by the standards of this file, *thin in primary footage and thick in inference*. The subject himself is the principal source: he refers to his parents, his upbringing, his faith, and his intended vocation in a pattern of brief, almost defensive disclosures across all four diegetic years. No family member appears on canonical footage. No phone calls are recorded. No home visits are documented. This absence is itself one of the most important data points in his entire file, and it organizes nearly every observation below: a young man raised, by his own description, in a warm and intact home has, since the Kilgrave compromise, conducted his life at a near-total operational distance from that home. The family is not *estranged* in the conventional sense — there is no rupture, no fight, no break — but it is *firewalled*. The reasons for the firewall, and the costs of maintaining it, are the substance of this section.

What follows treats each origin bond on its own terms: the mother, the father, the faith community as quasi-familial structure, the absent siblings (status unconfirmed and itself notable), and the dead — meaning here the pre-Kilgrave self, whom Malcolm mourns in language ordinarily reserved for a deceased family member, and who must be entered into this section because the subject treats him as kin he failed.

---

## The Mother

### History and texture

Malcolm's references to his mother are the warmest and the most carefully managed of any in his speech record. In Season 1, when first describing his background to other survivors and to Jessica, he names his mother before his father and lingers on her: she is described in terms suggesting daily presence, daily expectation, daily prayer. She is the parent who, by his account, most explicitly shaped his moral and vocational trajectory — the source of the imperative to *help*, to *witness*, and to *not waste* the relative stability of his upbringing. He does not name her profession on record, but the texture of his references (vocabulary around care, around obligation, around the moral weight of small daily kindnesses) reads as a household in which she occupied a caregiving or community-facing role, formally or informally.

### Dynamic

The relationship, premorbidly, is best characterized as **idealizing on both sides**. She had a high-achieving, gentle, articulate son who validated the family's values back to it. He had a mother whose approval was not coercively withheld but was *expected to be earned through the right kind of life* — and he was, by every indication, succeeding. There is no canonical evidence of conflict, rebellion, or adolescent rupture; the trajectory toward a clinical psychology graduate program is precisely the kind of choice that satisfies an expectant mother who has not had to demand satisfaction.

### Evolution post-Kilgrave

This is where the file becomes diagnostic. From the moment of the captivity forward, Malcolm's mother functions in his life almost exclusively as an **absent presence**: invoked in language, never in scene. He does not, as far as the record shows, return home during withdrawal. He does not bring her in to support the survivor group. He does not, at any documented juncture across four years, place a call to her in the presence of others, accept a visit from her, or travel to her. The subject who in S1 describes his mother with audible love conducts the rest of his observable life as though she lived on another continent.

The inference is unavoidable: **he is protecting her from what he has become, and protecting himself from her seeing it.** A mother who shaped him toward helping cannot be asked to look at a son who has been drugged, used as a surveillance tool, who has spent months photographing a stranger against his will, who is now working for an attorney whose ethics he privately detests. The premorbid contract — *I will be the good son you raised* — was broken by Kilgrave, and Malcolm has never re-presented himself to his mother under revised terms. He has, instead, presented her with silence, sanitized check-ins, and distance.

### Trust and safety

Trust toward the mother is structurally intact and emotionally untouched: she is, in his interior cartography, *safe*. But the safety is preserved precisely by non-contact. The relationship has been pickled in pre-Kilgrave amber. He trusts her unconditionally to be who she was when he last let her fully see him, which is to say, the version of her that lived with the version of him that no longer exists.

### Failure mode

The failure mode is **avoidant idealization**. If she were to appear unannounced in his S3 life — to witness the Hogarth work, the sexual instrumentalization, the bruises — the encounter would not produce repair; it would produce, in him, acute shame and likely a brittle, performative reassurance that would deepen his isolation rather than relieve it. He cannot currently tolerate being *seen by her*, which is the same thing as saying he cannot currently tolerate seeing himself through her eyes.

### Leverage

For a hostile operator: extreme. The invocation of his mother — her health, her safety, her judgment of him — is one of the few stimuli that would reliably override his hardened S2–S3 operational posture and revert him to the pre-Kilgrave moral grammar. A skilled manipulator could move him significantly with a falsified message, a staged emergency, or even a credible threat to her standing in her community.

For a benevolent handler or clinician: equally extreme, and in the opposite direction. Reconnection with the mother — supervised, paced, with permission for Malcolm to present himself as he actually is rather than as she expects — is among the highest-yield therapeutic interventions available. The block is in him, not her.

---

## The Father

### History and texture

The father appears in Malcolm's speech less frequently than the mother and in a different register — invoked more often as a *standard* than as a *presence*. Where the mother is the warmth, the father is the bar. Subject's references suggest a man of religious seriousness, occupational steadiness, and an expectation of dignity in conduct that was conveyed less through instruction than through example. There is no canonical evidence of harshness, no evidence of physical discipline, no evidence of withdrawal or absence; the picture is of a present, reliable, somewhat formal paternal figure whose love was expressed through provision, consistency, and the unspoken assumption that his son would carry the family's name well.

### Dynamic

Premorbidly: **respectful, dutiful, comparatively low-affect**. Malcolm's relationship to his father appears to have been the relationship of an admiring son to a man whose approval was real but quiet, and whose disapproval — never tested in any serious adolescent way — would have landed heavily because it was so rarely deployed. The vocational choice toward clinical psychology can be read partially through this lens: a helping profession that retains professional dignity, education, credentialing — a respectable form of the religious-moral imperative the father embodied.

### Evolution post-Kilgrave

The father is, if anything, *more* firewalled than the mother. Where the mother is invoked with warmth-at-distance, the father is invoked sparingly and with what reads as a faint flinch. The Kilgrave captivity produced exactly the kind of biography the father's worldview has no comfortable category for: a son who became a drug addict (irrespective of cause), who failed to protect a woman he was tasked with photographing, who left the seminary-adjacent trajectory of graduate clinical training for the morally murky world of private investigation, and who is now, by Season 3, doing things for a corporate attorney that would not survive being spoken aloud at his father's dinner table.

There is no canonical scene in which Malcolm explains any of this to his father. The inference is that he has not. The relationship has, like the maternal one, been preserved through a sanitized minimal-contact protocol — but where the mother is preserved as a warm idea, the father is preserved as a **standard not currently met**, and contact is correspondingly more painful and more avoided.

### Trust and safety

Trust is intact in the abstract — he does not believe his father would betray him, harm him, or fail him. *Safety*, however, is more compromised than with the mother. The father represents the moral mirror in which Malcolm cannot bear to see himself. Contact is therefore not unsafe in the sense of expected attack; it is unsafe in the sense of *expected accurate appraisal*.

### Failure mode

**Avoidance hardening into estrangement by attrition.** Neither party has ruptured the relationship; both are letting it thin through non-use. Without intervention, the most likely trajectory is that Malcolm's father will, at some point in the next decade, become someone Malcolm has not actually spoken to in any depth for years — at which point a precipitating event (illness, death, family crisis) will arrive into a relationship that has lost the muscle memory for difficult conversation. This is among the higher-probability future crises in the file.

### Leverage

Hostile: high but more brittle than with the mother. A direct threat to or invocation of the father would produce a sharper, less predictable response — possibly hardening rather than compliance, because the father's moral standard is part of what Malcolm is *trying to be worthy of by acting* rather than by retreating.

Benevolent: a structured paternal reconnection — particularly one that included the father's *witness* of Malcolm's actual current life rather than its sanitized version — would be transformative, but the activation energy required is among the highest of any therapeutic lever available. Malcolm would resist this reconnection more strenuously than almost any other intervention.

---

## The Faith Community as Quasi-Familial Structure

### History and texture

Malcolm's references to faith, while not exhaustive, are consistent and load-bearing. He was raised in a Black church tradition — the specific denomination is not canonically named but the affective and ethical signature (the moral seriousness, the orientation toward service, the framing of suffering within a redemptive narrative, the explicit anti-vengeance position he takes regarding Kilgrave) is unambiguous. The faith community functioned, premorbidly, as an extension of the family: a network of adults who knew him, expected things of him, and reinforced his parents' formation of him as a young man pointed toward helping work.

### Dynamic

This is best treated as a **quasi-familial origin bond** rather than as mere background context, because the file shows Malcolm's moral grammar throughout the four-year arc continuing to operate in the categories the church taught him — *witness*, *bear*, *forgive*, *do not return evil for evil*, *be of use*, *the worth of every person* — even when his behavior has drifted far from those categories. He argues against killing Kilgrave in explicitly faith-derived terms. He facilitates the survivor group in a register recognizable from pastoral care. He cannot quite stop himself from *naming his own moral state*, in S3, in the diction of confession.

### Evolution post-Kilgrave

Like the parents, the faith community is firewalled. He does not, on record, attend services in New York. He does not seek out a pastor. He does not appear to have a current congregational membership. He has retained the **moral software** without maintaining the **social hardware** that originally hosted it — which is part of why his moral injury accumulates: the framework that named his actions persists, but the community that would have absorbed, contextualized, and forgiven those actions is absent.

### Trust and safety

Trust toward the tradition itself remains; trust toward any *specific* current religious institution is unestablished, because he has not built one. He is, religiously speaking, a man holding his childhood church in his head and refusing to find an adult version of it.

### Failure mode

**Solo theology under chronic moral injury.** A faith framework designed to be practiced in community is being practiced alone, in fragments, mostly as self-judgment. This produces the worst features of religious moral seriousness (chronic guilt, scrupulosity, foreshortened self-forgiveness) without the best (absolution, communal witness, reintegration). The accumulation of S3 distress is partially attributable to this configuration.

### Leverage

Hostile: a manipulator with sufficient subtlety could weaponize his unattended faith — frame compromised acts as redemptive sacrifice, frame his moral fatigue as humility, frame continued service to a corrupt principal as a form of bearing-witness. He is vulnerable to this exact misuse because he has no current pastoral counterweight.

Benevolent: connection to a serious, ethically grounded religious community — particularly one with experience around trauma, addiction recovery, and moral injury — would provide the single most under-utilized resource in his entire support architecture.

---

## Siblings — Status Unconfirmed

### The file note

No sibling is named on canonical record. This is, in itself, a data point. Subjects raised in the cultural and religious configuration Malcolm describes — Black, middle-class, religious, intact two-parent household — statistically tend toward at least one sibling. The absence of any reference is consistent with three possibilities: (a) he is in fact an only child; (b) he has siblings whom he has so thoroughly firewalled from his current life that they do not arise even in self-disclosure; (c) there is a deceased or estranged sibling whose absence shapes him but whom he does not name.

### Inference

The behavioral evidence — particularly the intensity and rapidity with which he forms surrogate-sibling bonds with figures like Jessica (older-sister-coded), Trish (peer-coded), and the survivor group as a whole (lateral kin) — is consistent with someone who *does not have ready access to actual siblings* in his current life, whether because none exist or because they have been distanced. The hunger with which he constructs found-family in New York reads as compensatory for a lateral kin tier that is functionally empty.

### Trust, safety, failure mode, leverage

Cannot be assessed without confirmation. Flagged here as a high-priority intelligence gap; resolution of this gap would materially improve the predictive model of his attachment behavior.

---

## The Dead — The Pre-Kilgrave Self

### Justification for inclusion in this section

Standard practice would place this under Psychological. It is placed here because Malcolm's own behavior treats this prior self as **a deceased family member** rather than as a developmental stage. He grieves him. He invokes him. He measures his current conduct against him and finds it wanting. He has, in the language of the S3 arc, said versions of *I don't recognize myself* — which is the precise phenomenology of bereavement, not of self-criticism. The pre-Kilgrave Malcolm is, in his interior cartography, kin he failed to save, and the file is incomplete without registering this.

### History

The deceased: a young man, mid-twenties, recently relocated to New York, enrolled or about to enroll in graduate clinical training, faith-grounded, family-anchored, sexually and ethically inexperienced in any compromising sense, idealistic without being naive, warm, articulate, *intact*. This is the person Kilgrave selected, drugged, and used. This is the person who did not survive the captivity in any continuous sense.

### Dynamic

The surviving Malcolm maintains a complex relationship with the deceased Malcolm that includes: **mourning** (loss of the trajectory, the cleanness, the unbroken moral self); **guilt** (the irrational but persistent sense that he should have somehow prevented what was done to him); **idealization** (the deceased is remembered as morally cleaner than he probably was — premorbid Malcolm presumably had ordinary human failings that the captivity has now made him remember as virtues); and **intermittent resurrection attempts** (the survivor group, the moral stands in S1, the self-arresting recognition near the end of S3 are all attempts to behave as the deceased would have).

### Evolution

Across the four-year arc, the deceased's presence in Malcolm's daily operating mind appears to *decrease* in S2 (instrumental hardening reduces the friction of comparison) and *return sharply* in late S3 (the accumulation of compromise makes the comparison unavoidable). The grief is not linear; it is cyclic, triggered by moral-injury accumulation.

### Trust and safety

The deceased is the most trusted figure in Malcolm's interior. He is also the most dangerous, because the standard he sets is one the survivor cannot meet, and the gap between them is the engine of much of the suffering.

### Failure mode

**Pathological mourning expressed as self-punishment.** If Malcolm cannot find a way to integrate the deceased — to acknowledge that the pre-Kilgrave self is gone, that the current self is legitimate kin to him rather than his betrayer, and that the work of the rest of his life is *succession* rather than *resurrection* — he will continue to incur moral injury and continue to convert it into self-contempt.

### Leverage

Hostile: the most dangerous lever in the entire file. A manipulator who can credibly invoke the dead self — *the man you used to be would have done this* — can move Malcolm into actions he would otherwise refuse. The Hogarth dynamic in S3 partially exploits this without the operators knowing they are doing it.

Benevolent: the therapeutic task is, in essence, **a funeral**. He needs to be helped to bury the pre-Kilgrave self with honor, to mourn him without contempt for the survivor, and to begin a relationship with himself as he now is in which the dead man is held as ancestor rather than as accuser. No such work has been undertaken on record. It is the single most important intervention indicated by this section of the file.

---

## Section Synthesis

The origin bonds, taken together, share one structural feature that the rest of the file echoes at every level: **Malcolm Ducasse has preserved his family by not contacting them.** His mother, his father, his faith tradition, and possibly his siblings have all been protected from the post-Kilgrave version of him by his unilateral decision to keep them in pre-Kilgrave amber. This is, in the short term, a sophisticated defense — it preserves the *possibility* of return, it shields loved ones from secondary trauma, it keeps a clean moral reference point intact in his mind. In the long term it is corrosive, because the family that does not see him cannot welcome him back, and the welcome he most needs is precisely the one he is making structurally impossible to receive.

The dead self, meanwhile, is the family member with whom he is in *most* contact, and that contact is almost entirely accusatory. The architecture is therefore inverted: the living kin who could help him are firewalled, and the deceased kin who cannot help him is in his ear daily. Until this is reorganized — until the living are let back in and the dead is allowed to rest — the rest of his developmental trajectory will proceed under the conditions that produced his S3 drift, with predictable results.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

The subject's relational world is small, asymmetric, and concentrated in a single building (the Alias Investigations brownstone on West 46th) and a handful of adjacent professional orbits. He does not collect people; he attaches to a few and re-works those attachments past the point where most would have ended them. The pattern below is consistent across every figure on this list: he selects (or accepts being selected by) people who are simultaneously *demanding* and *withholding*, then earns his place through usefulness, and then experiences each rupture as confirmation of his post-Kilgrave conviction that he is welcome only as a function and not as a person. The fact that he keeps building relationships anyway is the most prosocial datum in the file.

---

## 1. JESSICA JONES — Primary Attachment, Rescuer, Employer, Pseudo-Sibling, Permanent Reference Point

**History.** First sustained contact occurs as predator-and-asset under Kilgrave's compulsion: the subject is photographing her from a stairwell across the hall while Kilgrave administers heroin to ensure compliance. She is, in those months, the object of his coerced surveillance and the only other adult who shares an apartment-floor with him. She knows him only as the wrecked addict neighbor who keeps trying to bum coffee. When she discovers the surveillance she does not kill him, which most analysts in this file would have predicted she might; she puts him through a near-fatal medical withdrawal in her own bathroom, watching him seize and sweat and vomit for approximately forty-eight hours. The subject names this, repeatedly, across four diegetic years, as the moment he was "given back" to himself.

**Dynamics.** From withdrawal onward the relationship is structured by an unrepayable debt the subject experiences as the central organizing fact of his adult life, and which she experiences as an irritant she cannot quite refuse. She is abrasive, intermittent, sarcastic, often drunk, and capable of long stretches of contemptuous indifference punctuated by sudden lethal loyalty. This reinforcement schedule — variable-ratio warmth against a baseline of abuse — is the most attachment-deepening pattern known to behavioral science, and it lands on a subject already primed by captivity to over-read intermittent kindness. He stays. He makes coffee. He answers her phone. He drags her drunk body up the stairs. He learns the rhythm of her bottle disposal and replaces the bourbon before she registers it is gone.

**Evolution.**
- *Phase 1 (post-withdrawal through end of S1):* unpaid auxiliary; runs the survivor support group partly as his own rehabilitation and partly as her perimeter defense. Trust is asymmetric: he tells her everything; she tells him almost nothing.
- *Phase 2 (early–mid S2):* de facto office manager and junior investigator. He learns surveillance, database work, lockpicking, and the social engineering at which he is, immediately, gifted. She acknowledges competence in clipped fragments; the subject metabolizes each fragment as a meal.
- *Phase 3 (late S2):* she fires him, ostensibly for over-reach, more accurately for closeness he was beginning to demand. He defects to Pryce Cheng within hours — the speed itself is diagnostic; he could not tolerate twenty-four hours of being merely abandoned without re-fitting himself into another structure.
- *Phase 4 (S3):* working for Hogarth, increasingly compromised, but the umbilical to Jessica never fully severs. He reappears at her crime scenes, her hospital corridors, her bar. Their last extended exchanges in the Salinger arc carry an unmistakable elegiac quality on both sides — a mutual acknowledgement that whatever they were is ending and that neither of them quite chose for it to.

**Trust / safety.** She is the only person to whom he has, on record, told the truth about the captivity year without translating it for an audience. Conversely she is the only person whose disapproval can produce in him something close to dissociative shame. *Functional safety:* very high — she will not betray him to outside parties and will, when pressed, defend him with violence disproportionate to her stated affection. *Emotional safety:* low — she punishes intimacy reflexively, often without intending the punitive effect.

**Failure mode.** He cannot register her withdrawals as data about *her* rather than verdicts about *him*. Every push becomes evidence in the running internal trial of his own worth. The S2 firing is the cleanest example: an outside observer reads "she's protecting you from her mess"; the subject reads "she has confirmed I am not enough," and the Cheng pivot follows within the night.

**Leverage.** Anyone wishing to move the subject can do so by routing through her: a credible threat to Jessica overrides every other consideration in his decision tree, including his own physical safety, his ethics, and his employment. Conversely, a credible signal that *Jessica needs him* will pull him out of any other commitment, including (S3 evidence) work for clients who pay him and respect him more.

---

## 2. TRISH WALKER — Lover, Co-Combatant, Cautionary Mirror

**History.** Introduced through Jessica's orbit. Initial impression on the subject is favorable: she is articulate, accomplished, publicly identified with survivor-advocacy themes that map onto his own caretaking grammar. Sexual relationship initiates mid-S2 under conditions that read, on the surface, as mutual attraction and, on closer reading, as instrumentally mixed — she wants information about Jessica's mother and uses sex as one of several levers; he allows himself to be used because being used by an attractive, demanding woman is, at that point in his post-captivity adaptation, the closest thing to feeling chosen.

**Dynamics.** The relationship is a study in two trauma profiles failing to recognize each other. She is escalating toward her own grandiosity-tinged crusade; he is in the early phase of instrumental hardening. Sexually they are compatible and even tender; relationally they consistently mis-time disclosure (she conceals; he over-shares and then resents the asymmetry). He notices, before most other characters do, that something is wrong with her — the appetite for risk, the willingness to lie to Jessica, the escalating self-reference of her crusade — and he names it to her, more than once, without effect.

**Evolution.** Bed partners → confidants of a limited and curated kind → estranged co-workers → adversaries by the end of S3, when her trajectory crosses Jessica's and the subject's loyalties resolve, predictably, toward Jessica. He grieves her in a low, private register that he does not show to Jessica, because to grieve Trish in Jessica's presence would be to claim a sorrow that, by the subject's internal ledger, he has not earned.

**Trust / safety.** Functional safety low throughout; she lies to him about her substances, her methods, and ultimately her acts. He is aware of this in real time and engages anyway, which is itself the diagnostic finding.

**Failure mode.** He confuses being *wanted* with being *known*, particularly when the wanting comes from a charismatic, troubled woman who reminds him, in tone if not in face, of Jessica. Trish is, in the subject's psychic economy, a Jessica-adjacent surrogate; the sexual relationship is, in part, an attempted side-channel transmission to the primary attachment.

**Leverage.** Diminished after S2; functionally zero by late S3. Memory of her, however, remains a usable lever — invocations of her early survivor-advocacy persona can briefly access the version of the subject who still believed proximity to public-good rhetoric was a measure of his own moral standing.

---

## 3. THE KILGRAVE SURVIVORS GROUP — Chosen Family, Clinical Laboratory, Self-Treatment Apparatus

**History.** Convened by the subject within weeks of his own withdrawal, in a borrowed church basement initially, then in the brownstone's ground-floor common space. Membership fluctuates; core attendees include other long-term coercion victims with overlapping symptom profiles. He facilitates without credentials, using a hybrid of his uncompleted clinical-psych training and the cadence of the AA meetings he sat through as a teenager when a relative was in recovery.

**Dynamics.** This group is the single most adaptive structure he builds in the entire record. It accomplishes four things simultaneously: (a) reasserts his pre-Kilgrave vocational identity; (b) provides a container for affect he cannot otherwise discharge; (c) gives him a population over whom he has appropriate, bounded authority — a corrective to the captivity in which all authority was the abuser's; (d) functions as a low-intensity surveillance network on Kilgrave-related developments, which Jessica uses without crediting.

**Evolution.** Active and central through S1. Attendance thins through S2 as members re-enter ordinary life or relapse into avoidance. By S3 the group has effectively dissolved as a regular meeting, though the subject maintains one-to-one contact with several former members on an as-needed basis. The dissolution is not failure; it is appropriate clinical course. Its absence in S3, however, removes one of his three principal regulators (Jessica, the group, the substance-free routine), which materially contributes to his moral drift.

**Trust / safety.** Highest of any relational structure in the file. The group members have, by definition, the one experiential reference the subject most needs witnessed. He could, in principle, say anything in that room. In practice he says less than the others, because facilitator role and self-disclosure compete, and he resolves the competition consistently in favor of the role.

**Failure mode.** He uses the group to treat himself by proxy and never seeks formal care in his own right. The structural cost is that when the group ends, the self-treatment ends with it, and there is no continuity of care into S2–S3.

**Leverage.** Any intervention that reactivates a facilitator role for him — small, prosocial, bounded — reliably moves him toward his pre-captivity baseline. Conversely, any attack on the group or its members in his presence will produce a response disproportionate to his usual conflict-avoidant baseline.

---

## 4. JERI HOGARTH — Employer, Patron, Anti-Mentor

**History.** Initial professional contact through Jessica. By S3 the subject is in Hogarth's direct employ on investigative and quasi-investigative matters, including the sustained Salinger work, witness handling, and personal-blackmail mitigation. He is paid better than at any prior point in his working life; he dresses better; his shoes are visibly new (a detail a surveillance file would note as a fidelity-of-corruption marker).

**Dynamics.** Hogarth is a precise, instrumentally brilliant, terminally ill, ethically eroded attorney whose tonal register — controlled, dry, faintly contemptuous — closely resembles the register the subject already attuned to under Jessica. The transference is rapid and largely unconscious. She gives him discrete, well-bounded tasks; he completes them; she compensates and re-tasks without elaboration. He experiences this clean transactional rhythm, after the chaos of late-S2 Jessica, as relief. Relief is the dangerous affect here: it lowers his ethical guard because the absence of friction reads, somatically, as safety.

**Evolution.** Subordinate-to-fixer over the course of S3. Each task is incrementally dirtier than the last: surveillance, then surveillance with intent to embarrass, then surveillance of a sexual partner with intent to coerce, then manipulation of a vulnerable witness. He narrates his discomfort at each step ("I don't recognize myself") and completes each step. By late S3 he is, in functional terms, a competent ethical-laundering apparatus for Hogarth's appetites. The recognition near series' end that this trajectory cannot continue is genuine but late.

**Trust / safety.** Functional safety moderate (she does not betray him, and pays); emotional safety zero — she does not see him as a person whose moral injury her work is incurring, and would, if asked, frame the relationship in purely contractual terms.

**Failure mode.** Hogarth is the file's clearest demonstration of the *accumulation effect* noted in the Psychiatric section. She does not ask him for any single act he would refuse cold; she asks him for seven acts in a sequence calibrated so each one is only slightly worse than the last. He is constitutionally vulnerable to exactly this titration.

**Leverage.** A handler who wished to move the subject toward conduct against his values should imitate Hogarth's method precisely: pay well, ask cleanly, escalate by half-degrees, never invoke higher principles, and reward competence verbally and economically without warmth. Conversely, breaking this dynamic requires an external intervention that names the sequence aloud — the subject's intellectualization defense will, once the pattern is articulated, finally serve his interests rather than insulate him from them.

---

## 5. PRYCE CHENG — Rival-Mentor, Tactical Employer, Misread Patron

**History.** Hires the subject within hours of the late-S2 firing by Jessica. The hiring is partly strategic on Cheng's part (he is in active commercial conflict with Alias Investigations and the defection is valuable signaling) and partly opportunistic (the subject is genuinely talented and currently undervalued). The subject accepts because, as noted, twenty-four hours without an attachment structure was intolerable.

**Dynamics.** Cheng runs a larger, more corporate, more conventionally professional shop than Jessica's. The subject, for the first time, experiences scaled investigative work with proper resources, colleagues, a desk, salary structure, and benefits. He is briefly, visibly happier in the surface register and quietly miserable underneath. He is unable to convert the structural improvement into felt satisfaction because the underlying loss (Jessica's regard) is not addressed by it.

**Evolution.** Brief but instructive. The relationship does not survive the next phase of Jessica's crisis; the subject re-routes attention and partial loyalty back toward the brownstone within weeks of departure. Cheng is appropriately cynical about this and largely lets him go.

**Trust / safety.** Functional safety high (corporate norms, contractual clarity); emotional safety irrelevant — neither party invests in that register.

**Failure mode.** He cannot use a healthy professional structure when his attachment system is unsettled. The Cheng episode is the cleanest evidence that the subject's career problems are not, in fact, career problems.

**Leverage.** Limited. Cheng is one of the few figures in the record from whom the subject can walk away without significant internal cost, which makes Cheng atypical and also makes him a useful diagnostic baseline: anything the subject does for Cheng but not for Jessica or Hogarth is, by elimination, what he can do *without* coercion or attachment hunger driving him.

---

## 6. RUBEN — Murdered Neighbor, Unmetabolized Grief

**History.** Brief but consequential. The young neighbor in the brownstone, a guileless and somewhat lost figure who orbited Jessica with a doomed crush, killed by Kilgrave during the S1 arc as a manipulative gesture toward Jessica. The subject knew him in the casual, hallway-and-stairwell register of New York apartment life and, in the post-Kilgrave clarity, recognized in him a version of his own pre-captivity self: earnest, undefended, in over his head.

**Dynamics.** Largely posthumous. Ruben functions in the subject's interior life as a *what-if-I-had-not-been-rescued* counterfactual and, simultaneously, as a member of the implicit roster of people whose deaths were collateral to the captivity year. The subject does not speak of him often, which is, given the subject's typical verbal processing style, itself the indicator that the grief is encysted rather than digested.

**Trust / safety.** Not applicable in present tense; relevant only as ghost.

**Failure mode.** Unprocessed; contributes a steady low-grade pull on the subject's already overdetermined sense of survivor responsibility.

**Leverage.** Any operator with access to detail about Ruben's death could use it to access guilt unreachable through other channels. Therapeutically, the same vector would be among the highest-yield disclosures available in formal treatment, if the subject ever entered it.

---

## 7. THE FAMILY OF ORIGIN (PARENTS, PRESUMED SIBLINGS, FAITH COMMUNITY) — Distant Anchor, Unvisited Ground

**History.** Established in expository reference: intact, religious, warm, Black middle-class, located outside the New York frame of canonical record. The move to New York for graduate study is the precipitating circumstance of his isolation, which Kilgrave then exploits.

**Dynamics.** Across the four-year record there is no documented in-person visit home, no parent appearing in the apartment, no sibling on screen, no pastor on the phone. There are references — he carries the moral grammar of the upbringing audibly in his speech — but the actual people are absent. This absence is the single largest unaccounted variable in the file. Either he is protecting them from what he has become, or they are protecting themselves from what he has become, or both.

**Trust / safety.** Presumed high in principle, untested in practice. The subject's avoidance is, on its face, protective: telling his parents about the captivity year would force a confrontation with the gap between the son they raised and the man currently working for Hogarth.

**Failure mode.** Disclosure deferred indefinitely. Each year of deferral makes return harder, because return now requires accounting for not only the original trauma but also every subsequent compromise. By late S3 the deferral has become its own secondary moral injury.

**Leverage.** Highest single recoverable lever in the file. The faith-and-family register can recall him to his pre-Kilgrave self more rapidly than any other intervention; it can also, weaponized, produce shame deep enough to immobilize him. Any handler — therapeutic or hostile — with access to this register has access to the deepest part of him.

---

## 8. THE BROWNSTONE ITSELF — Place as Chosen Family

A note on a non-human "ally," in the surveillance-file sense. The subject's relationship to the brownstone on West 46th is, by the end of the record, indistinguishable from a relationship with a person. He knows its stair-creaks, its lock idiosyncrasies, the angle of light into the hallway in October, the radiator that knocks at 5:40 a.m., the precise volume at which Jessica's apartment door slams when she is angry versus when she is merely drunk. He has lived nowhere else of consequence in the canonical record. The building is the physical container of the worst and best things that have happened to him — the captivity-era apartment is doors away from the withdrawal-bathroom is doors away from the survivor-group room is doors away from Jessica's office. He could move; he does not. Place attachment of this density, in a subject otherwise mobile and resourced, indicates that the building is performing a holding function he has not otherwise secured. Removing him from it would be a higher-impact intervention than removing him from any single person on this list except Jessica.

---

## SYNTHESIS — THE SHAPE OF THE CIRCLE

The subject's allied world is a tight, attachment-saturated, geographically concentrated arrangement of a primary figure (Jessica), an anti-mentor (Hogarth), a brief tactical employer (Cheng), a sexual-and-political mirror (Trish), a self-treatment collective (the survivor group), a ghost (Ruben), an absent anchor (the family of origin), and a building. Nearly every relationship was either initiated under coercion (Jessica, posthumously Ruben), brokered through Jessica (Trish, Hogarth), or selected as Jessica's counterweight (Cheng). The orbital center of the entire system is therefore the primary attachment, and any modeling of the subject's behavior that does not weight her presence above all other factors will mis-predict.

The most prosocial datum in this section is that despite a relational map dominated by figures who use him, the subject has not closed. He still convenes. He still answers the phone. He still drags drunk women up stairs. He still, at the door of his own moral undoing, asks the question — *do I recognize myself?* — that opens, rather than closes, the next conversation. He has been taught, by every important figure in this list except his absent family, that he is welcome as a function. He behaves, persistently, as if he believes he is welcome as a person. He is not yet correct about that. The work of any future ally, mentor, or clinician is to make him correct.


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

*The third tranche of the relationship dossier. Tranche I mapped family and origin attachments; Tranche II mapped allies, colleagues, and the surrogate-family cluster (Jessica, Trish, Hogarth in her benign register). This tranche treats the figures who hurt him, opposed him, or shared a bed with him - frequently overlapping categories in this subject's case. Sub-sections are ordered by causal centrality to the behavioral record, not chronologically.*

---

## I. RIVALS & ENEMIES

### 1. Kevin Thompson / "Kilgrave" — the originating injury

**History.** Encountered in the first quarter of the diegetic record, approximately eighteen months before the canonical opening of *Jessica Jones* S1. The encounter is non-random: Kilgrave selects the subject in part because he is conspicuously alone in a new city, conspicuously idealistic (the graduate-school trajectory was legible from his bearing, his clothes, his routes), and conspicuously susceptible to a smiling stranger with a request. The opening compulsion is small - a photograph, a follow, a return. Within weeks the subject is being administered heroin under instruction; within months he is a daily user, photographing Jessica Jones from windows and doorways across her block and reporting at intervals. The relationship lasts roughly twelve to eighteen months in this form and ends only when Jessica identifies him as the surveillance asset and physically extracts him.

**Dynamic.** The defining feature of this dyad is that the subject is not, in any meaningful sense, a participant. Kilgrave's power obliterates the volitional substrate of relationship. What he experiences, however, is participation - the phenomenology of choosing, wanting, complying, even pleasing - because Kilgrave's compulsions are felt from the inside as one's own intentions. This is the cruelest signature of the harm: the subject was not merely used; he was made to experience his own use as desire. Every subsequent symptom flows from this fact.

**Evolution.** Post-extraction, the relationship persists as internal occupation. Kilgrave's voice and presence recur as intrusive material for at least the first canonical year and detectably (in micro-tells: a stiffening at the sight of purple, an aversion to British-accented men of a certain register, a refusal to take pills offered by anyone other than a pharmacist) thereafter. The subject's S1 advocacy against killing Kilgrave is overdetermined: faith-based, yes, but also a refusal to let Kilgrave make him a killer, which would be the final compulsion. When Kilgrave is killed by Jessica, the subject's reaction is not relief but a complicated quiet - the abuser's death does not return the year, and the survivor knows it.

**Trust/safety.** Zero, asymptotically. He never trusted Kilgrave because trust was not the operative mechanism. What Kilgrave installed instead is a permanent baseline mistrust of *his own wants* - a metacognitive scar that surfaces whenever he notices himself wanting something strongly (a job, a woman, a drink, a confrontation), in which the question *is this mine* runs as a background process he cannot turn off.

**Failure mode.** Re-enactment. The subject's S2-S3 drift can be read as a serial attempt to occupy, voluntarily, the position Kilgrave forced on him - to be the one who surveils, who manipulates, who breaks consent for instrumental ends - on the unconscious theory that doing it freely cancels having had it done. It does not cancel it. It compounds it.

**Leverage (against the subject).** Anything that recreates the Kilgrave signature: orders without explanation, smiling charm with concealed coercion, substances offered without provenance, sexual situations in which his consent is assumed rather than secured, surveillance directed at people he cares about. A skilled operator who studied the Kilgrave playbook could move the subject into compliance and then watch him punish himself for it - a self-executing trap.

---

### 2. Will Simpson — the proximate threat, the warning

**History.** Encountered late S1 as Trish Walker's lover, initially presenting as a competent and stabilizing partner, deteriorating across the arc into combat-stimulant-driven paranoid aggression. The subject's dealings with him are limited in screen-minutes but disproportionate in psychological weight.

**Dynamic.** Simpson is the first figure the subject meets, post-Kilgrave, who is also a chemically altered instrument of someone else's purpose. The structural parallel registers. The subject's wariness of Simpson is immediate and physical - a different muscle tonus in the room, shorter sentences, eyes tracking hands. He understands earlier than anyone else present that Simpson is already gone.

**Evolution.** Brief. Simpson is removed from the field by the end of the season. The relationship's afterlife is entirely internal: Simpson becomes a cautionary template the subject carries forward - *this is what happens when a person uses a substance to outrun their helplessness* - which is precisely the diagnosis he should be applying to himself in S2-S3 and is not.

**Trust/safety.** None operationally; some reluctant sympathy. The subject can hold both - he knows Simpson is dangerous and that Simpson is also, in the analytically relevant sense, a fellow casualty.

**Failure mode.** The subject's failure with Simpson is one of pattern-blindness applied inward. He sees in Simpson exactly what he refuses to see in himself: a man who replaced his shattered agency with a chemical and behavioral prosthesis and called it strength.

**Leverage.** Negligible. Simpson is too unstable to be a sustained antagonist and the subject too peripheral to his agenda to be a sustained target.

---

### 3. Pryce Cheng — the rival who became the employer

**History.** Cheng surfaces in S2 as the proprietor of a larger, more polished, more aggressively commercial investigations firm explicitly attempting to absorb or destroy Alias Investigations. He attempts to recruit Jessica, is rebuffed, escalates, is beaten. Some weeks later, when Jessica fires or sidelines the subject, Cheng hires him. The transit from rival to employer occurs across approximately the run of an episode and a half.

**Dynamic.** The subject's defection to Cheng is the first major behavioral inflection of his post-Kilgrave adult life and warrants the granularity. Surface read: pragmatic career move by a competent operator whose mentor has rejected him. Closer read: a defended, retaliatory, attachment-driven act dressed in the clothes of professionalism. He chooses Cheng because Cheng is everything Jessica is not - structured, well-resourced, transactionally clear, willing to value him explicitly. The subject is consciously aware of perhaps thirty percent of this.

The working relationship is functional. Cheng is not cruel; he is corporate. He pays on time, provides equipment, assigns cases with clear deliverables, and does not require the subject to absorb his moods. After Jessica's intermittent reinforcement, this feels, briefly, like adulthood. It is also psychologically inert in a way that bores the subject within weeks: Cheng cannot give him the one currency he needs, which is being *seen by someone who knows what happened to him*.

**Evolution.** The arrangement does not last. The subject returns to Jessica's orbit and to work that matters to him, with Cheng receding to a professional acquaintance.

**Trust/safety.** Moderate operational trust, low intimate trust. Cheng is exactly as reliable as the contract specifies and no more, and the subject reads this correctly throughout.

**Failure mode.** The subject mistook *clarity* for *care* during a period when he was starved for care and could not tolerate that fact. He purchased a temporary stabilization by stepping outside the relational system that was actually his life, and the price was a measurable acceleration of his ethical drift, since Cheng's work normalized the kind of investigative practice (rougher, more surveillance-forward, more billable-hour-rationalized) that the subject would carry into the Hogarth period.

**Leverage.** As a former employer Cheng could call him back into contract work and would likely receive a polite refusal; as an antagonist Cheng has no purchase on the subject's inner life. The leverage flows the other way - the subject knows enough about Cheng's operational methods to be a credible threat if it came to that, though he would not exercise the threat.

---

### 4. Jeri Hogarth — patron, employer, corrupter

*Filed here rather than in Tranche II because, on the evidence, the relationship's net effect on the subject is closer to that of an enemy than an ally, irrespective of intent.*

**History.** Initially encountered as Jessica's lawyer in S1; becomes the subject's direct employer in S3, retaining him for personal extralegal work concurrent with her ALS diagnosis and her pursuit of the blackmailer who scammed her. The arrangement is well-compensated, ostensibly autonomous, and progressively degrading.

**Dynamic.** Hogarth is the most sophisticated handler the subject has encountered. She does not order; she requests, with the implication that a competent professional would already have anticipated the request. She does not coerce; she creates situations in which refusal would constitute a personal failure rather than a moral stand. She titrates the compromises - a surveillance, then a sexual-surveillance, then an intimidation, then a manipulation of a witness whose vulnerability is exactly the sort the subject was trained to protect - and at each step the increment is small enough that the cost of refusal exceeds the cost of compliance. This is precisely the operational profile the subject's psychology cannot resist: accumulation, not confrontation.

The subject knows. He says so, aloud, multiple times. The knowing does not stop the doing. This is the diagnostic signature of moral injury rather than moral failure: the values are intact and the behavior diverges from them anyway.

**Evolution.** From respectful professional engagement to compromised retainer to self-recognizing departure. By the late S3 arc, the subject's affect in Hogarth's presence has changed - shorter responses, less eye contact, a flatter professional warmth that reads, to a careful observer, as a man preparing to leave.

**Trust/safety.** He trusts her competence absolutely and her ethics not at all. He is correct on both counts. She is physically safe to be around; psychologically she is the most dangerous person in his post-Kilgrave file because she operates on him in exactly the modality he is least equipped to resist.

**Failure mode.** He let her hire the parts of him that Kilgrave had already trained - the surveillant, the compliant operator, the man who completes assignments he disapproves of - and let her pay him well enough that he could call it work rather than re-enactment.

**Leverage (against the subject).** Knowledge of his S3 conduct. Hogarth holds receipts on tasks the subject performed that would, if disclosed, end any prospect of a return to clinical-counseling work and would devastate his standing with his family of origin. She is not, on the evidence, the sort of person who would deploy this lightly; she is exactly the sort who would deploy it if cornered. Permanent latent threat.

---

### 5. Gregory Salinger — the antagonist who saw him

**History.** Encountered as the principal antagonist of S3 - a high-functioning predator whose methodology relies on identifying the moral self-concept of his targets and unstitching it from the inside. The subject is involved in the investigation and confrontation across the back half of the season.

**Dynamic.** Salinger is the only antagonist after Kilgrave who *looks at the subject and sees the subject*, and what he sees, accurately, is a man whose drift he can name. Salinger's particular sadism is the diagnostic monologue; the subject is, briefly, on the receiving end of a reading that aligns disturbingly well with what an honest clinician would have said about him at that point. The subject's revulsion is partly moral and partly the revulsion of being seen by the wrong observer.

**Evolution.** Brief, intense, terminal. The relationship does not persist because Salinger does not persist; what persists is the residue of having been correctly described by an enemy.

**Trust/safety.** None. Salinger is the second person in the subject's life whose presence in a room reorganizes his nervous system, and the subject knows it within the first conversation.

**Failure mode.** The subject's failure with Salinger is the failure to convert the encounter, in the moment, into the self-recognition it ought to have triggered. He treats Salinger's reading as a hostile maneuver to be deflected rather than as a hostile maneuver that happens to be true. The deflection costs him weeks of the recognition he eventually achieves anyway.

**Leverage.** Salinger had, while alive, the leverage of accurate description. Post-mortem, none.

---

### 6. The diffuse enemy class — Kilgrave's network of compelled actors, IGH personnel, sundry criminal-adjacent figures encountered through casework

A generalized note rather than a sub-section. The subject's enemy file across four canonical years is otherwise populated by short-encounter figures - thugs, marks, witnesses turned hostile, mid-tier criminals - none of whom rise to relational significance. His professional risk-acceptance with this class is notable: he absorbs beatings with a placidity that reads, in the file, as not-quite-suicidal indifference to his own body. This is a behavioral pattern, not a relationship, and is treated elsewhere in the file. Logged here only because the cumulative effect of these encounters is the slow normalization of physical risk that supports the broader ethical drift.

---

## II. LOVERS & INTIMATES

A short list. The subject's sexual and romantic life across the canonical window is sparse, episodic, and over-determined by his trauma. There are two figures of substance and a category of brief encounters worth naming.

### 1. Robyn — the neighbor, the entanglement, the rebound that wasn't

**History.** Resident of the same building as the subject and as Ruben, Robyn's twin brother. Encountered through proximity in S1; the relationship begins after Ruben's murder by Kilgrave, with the subject acting first as a caretaker to Robyn's grief (his default mode) and then, with a permeability characteristic of his post-Kilgrave attachment style, as her lover. The sexual relationship is intermittent across the back half of S1 and into S2, conducted without formal commitment and with mutual ambivalence.

**Dynamic.** This is, on close inspection, not a romance. It is a caretaking arrangement with a sexual component, in which the subject extends the survivor-group facilitation modality into bed. Robyn is loud, demanding, grief-shattered, and prone to attaching with disproportionate intensity to anyone who tolerates her; she finds in the subject someone who will tolerate her indefinitely. He finds in her a person whose need is so overt that meeting it requires no introspection on his part - he can be useful without being known. The arrangement suits the part of him that wants to avoid being known.

**Evolution.** Dissolves by attrition rather than rupture. As the subject professionalizes into investigative work and his world enlarges, Robyn's centripetal demands become incompatible with his trajectory. There is no explicit parting; there is a tapering, then a polite distance.

**Trust/safety.** Physically safe; emotionally negligible in either direction. He never discloses the Kilgrave year to her in substance; she never asks in a way that would invite disclosure. The relationship is, by mutual unstated agreement, structured around what it does not address.

**Failure mode.** The subject used Robyn as a regulator. The sex was a regulator, the caretaking was a regulator, the very ambient irritation of her presence was a regulator - all of it provided enough affective texture to keep him from sitting alone with what had happened to him. This is a misuse of another person, and a competent clinician would name it as such. The fact that Robyn benefited from the same misuse in the opposite direction does not redeem either party's conduct; it merely explains why neither protested.

**Leverage.** None operationally extant. She does not know what he is. The latent leverage of having had a sexual partner who could, in principle, describe him to others is negligible because the descriptions she could offer are not the ones that would damage him.

---

### 2. Trish Walker — the transactional intimacy, the line he should not have crossed

**History.** Long-running ally of Jessica's, encountered repeatedly across S1-S3. The single recorded sexual encounter occurs in S2 under conditions of mutual instrumentality - she wants information or access she believes he can obtain; he is in a period of acute Jessica-related rejection sensitivity and is available to be wanted by anyone in Jessica's orbit. The encounter is brief and not repeated.

**Dynamic.** The most diagnostically revealing intimate event in the file. The subject permits himself to be used sexually for instrumental purposes by a woman he knows is using him, and he is aware in real time of the dynamic. He does not protest, negotiate, or refuse. He converts his body into a transactional asset, exactly as Kilgrave once did. The re-enactment is precise enough that it is difficult to read as coincidence, and the absence of a meaningful self-protective response is itself the symptom.

**Evolution.** The encounter is functionally archived rather than processed. He and Trish continue to occupy the same social orbit without recurrence and without explicit acknowledgement; the residue is a slight stiffness in their interactions that an observer would have to be looking for to catch.

**Trust/safety.** He trusts Trish less after the encounter than before, accurately, but he does not generalize from it; he files it as a Trish-specific event rather than as the data it actually is about himself.

**Failure mode.** He treated his own consent as a low-value resource freely spendable in pursuit of any minimally plausible objective. This is the sexual-domain expression of his broader instrumental hardening, and it is the closest he comes, on record, to participating in his own violation.

**Leverage.** Mutual and stable. Each holds the encounter as a thing not discussed; neither has incentive to deploy it.

---

### 3. The category of brief encounters — bar contacts, casework adjacencies, undocumented partners

A note rather than a sub-section. The S2-S3 arc includes scattered evidence of casual sexual contact that does not rise to relational status: late returns to the apartment, occasional unfamiliar items in his space, the affective signature of a man who is having sex he is not invested in. This is not pathology in itself; in the context of his profile it is one more regulator, structurally identical in function to the Robyn arrangement, scaled down to single encounters because by S3 he has neither the energy nor the inclination for arrangements. Logged for completeness.

---

### 4. Jessica Jones — filed elsewhere, referenced here

The most consequential intimate relationship in the file is non-sexual and is addressed in Tranche II under surrogate-family attachments. It is named here only to record that the subject's romantic and sexual life across four years is partly explicable as a series of substitutions for, or evasions of, the one relationship in which he is fully known and which he cannot safely deepen. Every lover he takes is, in part, a person Jessica is not. This does not mean he wants Jessica romantically in any straightforward sense; it means the attachment hunger Kilgrave installed has only one address that recognizes the package, and every other delivery is returned.

---

## III. CROSS-CUTTING OBSERVATIONS

**The shape of the enemy file.** The subject's antagonists, taken together, form a remarkably coherent typology: each is a figure who can or does override the volition of others, whether by superpower (Kilgrave), chemical alteration (Simpson), corporate-legal sophistication (Hogarth, Cheng), or diagnostic predation (Salinger). He does not collect enemies at random; he collects the same enemy in successive forms, because the original enemy was never resolved and the psyche keeps re-presenting the problem until it is. A handler reading this file should expect the subject to continue magnetizing this class of figure until formal treatment intervenes.

**The shape of the lover file.** Conversely, his intimate partners are, almost without exception, people with whom genuine mutual knowing is structurally impossible - either because the partner does not want to know him (Trish, the brief encounters) or because the partner cannot bear what knowing him would require (Robyn). The lover file is the photographic negative of the Jessica relationship: every entry is defined by the absence of what that one relationship contains.

**Composite leverage profile.** A hostile actor with access to this tranche would target the subject through (a) recreation of the Kilgrave signature in any modality, (b) deployment of the Hogarth-held documentary residue of his S3 conduct, (c) exploitation of his demonstrated willingness to spend his body and consent as transactional assets, or (d) introduction of a charismatic figure offering the seen-and-known experience that only Jessica currently supplies. The fourth vector is the most dangerous because the subject would walk into it without resistance and might not recognize the trap until he was inside it.

**Composite protective profile.** Conversely, a benevolent intervention would: refuse to issue him orders without explanation, refuse to accept his services as a substitute for his presence, name the Kilgrave-shaped pattern in his current relationships when it appears, and decline to be useful to him in ways that permit him to continue avoiding his own treatment. The list of people on record who have done any of these things for him is, at present, empty.


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS

## Preliminary Framing

Malcolm Ducasse is, in the technical sense, an **un-powered subject operating in a powered ecology**. He has no enhancements, no augmentations, no metahuman physiology, no specialized training of the martial or paramilitary kind. Everything in this section is therefore an inventory of *acquired, ordinary human capacities* — and the analytic interest lies precisely in how a wholly baseline subject becomes, across forty-odd months of canon, a materially effective investigator and field operator alongside enhanced individuals. His "abilities" are a compound of (a) premorbid intellectual gifts, (b) trauma-forged tolerances, and (c) on-the-job apprenticeship under a powered investigator. Each item below should be read as both a *capacity* and a *symptom*: what he can do is, almost without exception, traceable to what was done to him or to what he was before it was done.

---

## 1. Clinical-Psychological Perception (the foundational ability)

**Mechanism.** Pre-Kilgrave coursework in psychology and counseling, plus a native temperamental disposition to read affect, motive, and incongruity in others. He scans interlocutors the way a trained clinician scans a patient: tracking microexpression, vocal prosody, pause length, eye-line breaks, hand position, the gap between stated and apparent affect. He formulates in real time — silently constructing a working etiology of whoever he is talking to.

**Range.** Reliable in dyadic, in-person interactions; degrades over phone, further over text. He is particularly accurate on **trauma signatures** in others — a survivor's recognition of survivors — which is why his facilitation of the Kilgrave Survivors group is so effective so quickly. He correctly clocks Trish's compulsive striving, Hogarth's terror beneath her control, Cheng's brittleness beneath his bluster, and (intermittently) Jessica's grief beneath her contempt.

**Limits.** His perception is excellent; his *action* on perception is compromised by attachment hunger and by the reaction-formation against helplessness catalogued in Section 3. He sees Jessica's manipulation of him in S2 and complies anyway. He sees Hogarth's instrumentalization of him in S3 and complies anyway. The bottleneck is never the read — it is the willingness to act on the read against an attachment figure's wishes.

**Signature application.** The survivors' group, where he gets to use the perception cleanly and prosocially, with no attachment-figure interference. He is, in that room, what he was supposed to be.
